Chapter 2: THREE OLD LADIES KNIT THE SOCKS OF DEATH

I WAS USED TO THE OCCASIONAL WEIRD experience, but usually they were over quickly. This twenty-four/seven hallucination was more than I could handle. For the rest of the school year, the entire campus seemed to be playing some kind of trick on me. The students acted as if they were completely and totally convinced that Mrs. Kerr—a perky blond woman whom I‚Äôd never seen in my life until she got on our bus at the end of the field trip—had been our pre-algebra teacher since Christmas.

I started feeling cranky and irritable most of the time. My grades slipped from Ds to Fs. I got into more fights with Nancy Bobofit and her friends. I was sent out into the hallway in almost every class.

CHAPTER 6: I BECOME SUPREME LORD OF THE BATHROOM

ONCE I GOT OVER THE FACT THAT my Latin teacher was a horse, we had a nice tour, though I was careful not to walk behind him. I‚Äôd done pooper-scooper patrol in the Macy‚Äôs Thanksgiving Day Parade a few times, and, I‚Äôm sorry, I did not trust Chiron‚Äôs back end the way I trusted his front.

Most of the campers were older than me. I looked back at the farmhouse. It was a lot bigger than I‚Äôd realized— four stories tall, sky blue with white trim, like an upscale seaside resort. ‚ÄúWhat‚Äôs up there?‚Äù I asked Chiron.

‚ÄúCome along, Percy,‚Äù Chiron said, his lighthearted tone now a little forced. ‚ÄúLots to see.‚Äù

I watched the satyr playing his pipe. His music was causing lines of bugs to leave the strawberry patch in every direction, like refugees fleeing a fire. I wondered if Grover could work that kind of magic with music. I wondered if he was still inside the farmhouse, getting chewed out by Mr. D.

‚ÄúGrover won‚Äôt get in too much trouble, will he?‚Äù I asked Chiron. ‚ÄúI mean‚Ä¶he was a good protector. Really.‚Äù

‚ÄúBut he did that!‚Äù

WORD OF THE BATHROOM INCIDENT spread immediately. Wherever I went, campers pointed at me and murmured something about toilet water. Or maybe they were just staring at Annabeth, who was still pretty much dripping wet.

‚ÄúAnd my dad?‚Äù

Back at cabin eleven, everybody was talking and horsing around, waiting for dinner. ‚ÄúI don‚Äôt belong here,‚Äù I said. ‚ÄúI don‚Äôt even believe in gods.‚Äù

‚ÄúThe wing-footed messenger guy.‚Äù

‚ÄúThat‚Äôs him. Messengers. Medicine. Travelers, merchants, thieves. Anybody who uses the roads. That‚Äôs why you‚Äôre here, enjoying cabin eleven‚Äôs hospitality. Hermes isn‚Äôt picky about who he sponsors.‚Äù

I figured Luke didn‚Äôt mean to call me a nobody. He just had a lot on his mind.

‚ÄúYou ever meet your dad?‚Äù I asked.

‚ÄúOnce.‚Äù

I waited, thinking that if he wanted to tell me, he‚Äôd tell me. Apparently, he didn‚Äôt. I wondered if the story had anything to do with how he got his scar.

‚ÄúSomebody special?‚Äù

The whole cabin, about twenty of us, filed into the commons yard. We lined up in order of seniority, so of course I was dead last. We marched up the hill to the mess hall pavilion. Satyrs joined us from the meadow. Naiads emerged from the canoeing lake. In all, there were maybe a hundred campers, a few dozen satyrs, and a dozen assorted wood nymphs and naiads.

At the pavilion, torches blazed around the marble columns. A central fire burned in a bronze brazier the size of a bathtub. Each cabin had its own table, covered in white cloth trimmed in purple. Everybody else raised their glasses. ‚ÄúTo the gods!‚Äù

I said, ‚ÄúCherry Coke.‚Äù

The glass filled with sparkling caramel liquid.

Then I had an idea. ‚ÄúBlue Cherry Coke.‚Äù

The soda turned a violent shade of cobalt.

I took a cautious sip. Perfect.

I drank a toast to my mother.

She‚Äôs not gone, I told myself. Not permanently, anyway. She‚Äôs in the Underworld. And if that‚Äôs a real place, then someday‚Ä¶

‚ÄúHere you go, Percy,‚Äù Luke said, handing me a platter of smoked brisket.

I loaded my plate and was about to take a big bite when I noticed everybody getting up, carrying their plates toward the fire in the center of the pavilion. I wondered if they were going for dessert or something.

‚ÄúCome on,‚Äù Luke told me.

Luke murmured in my ear, ‚ÄúBurnt offerings for the gods. They like the smell.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou‚Äôre kidding.‚Äù

His look warned me not to take this lightly, but I couldn‚Äôt help wondering why an immortal, all-powerful being would like the smell of burning food.

I was next.

I wished I knew what god‚Äôs name to say.

Finally, I made a silent plea. Whoever you are, tell me. Please.

I scraped a big slice of brisket into the flames.

When I caught a whiff of the smoke, I didn‚Äôt gag.

It smelled nothing like burning food. It smelled of hot chocolate and fresh-baked brownies, hamburgers on the grill and wildflowers, and a hundred other good things that shouldn‚Äôt have gone well together, but did. I could almost believe the gods could live off that smoke.

‚ÄúPersonally,‚Äù Mr. D continued, ‚ÄúI couldn‚Äôt care less, but congratulations. Also, I should tell you that we have a new camper today. Peter Johnson.‚Äù

‚ÄúEr, Percy Jackson,‚Äù Mr. D corrected. ‚ÄúThat‚Äôs right. Hurrah, and all that. Now run along to your silly campfire. Go on.‚Äù

Everybody cheered. We all headed down toward the amphitheater, where Apollo‚Äôs cabin led a sing-along. We sang camp songs about the gods and ate s‚Äômores and joked around, and the funny thing was, I didn‚Äôt feel that anyone was staring at me anymore. I felt that I was home.

Later in the evening, when the sparks from the campfire were curling into a starry sky, the conch horn blew again, and we all filed back to our cabins. I didn‚Äôt realize how exhausted I was until I collapsed on my borrowed sleeping bag.

My fingers curled around the Minotaur‚Äôs horn. I thought about my mom, but I had good thoughts: her smile, the bedtime stories she would read me when I was a kid, the way she would tell me not to let the bedbugs bite.

When I closed my eyes, I fell asleep instantly.

That was my first day at Camp Half-Blood.

I wish I‚Äôd known how briefly I would get to enjoy my new home.

CHAPTER 8: WE CAPTURE A FLAG

THE NEXT FEW DAYS I SETTLED INTO a routine that felt almost normal, if you don‚Äôt count the fact that I was getting lessons from satyrs, nymphs, and a centaur.

Each morning I took Ancient Greek from Annabeth, and we talked about the gods and goddesses in the present tense, which was kind of weird. I discovered Annabeth was right about my dyslexia: Ancient Greek wasn‚Äôt that hard for me to read. At least, no harder than English. After a couple of mornings, I could stumble through a few lines of Homer without too much headache.

The rest of the day, I‚Äôd rotate through outdoor activities, looking for something I was good at. Chiron tried to teach me archery, but we found out pretty quick I wasn‚Äôt any good with a bow and arrow. He didn‚Äôt complain, even when he had to desnag a stray arrow out of his tail.

Foot racing? No good either. The wood-nymph instructors left me in the dust. They told me not to worry about it. They‚Äôd had centuries of practice running away from lovesick gods. But still, it was a little humiliating to be slower than a tree.

And wrestling? Forget it. Every time I got on the mat, Clarisse would pulverize me.

‚ÄúThere‚Äôs more where that came from, punk,‚Äù she‚Äôd mumble in my ear.

The only thing I really excelled at was canoeing, and that wasn‚Äôt the kind of heroic skill people expected to see from the kid who had beaten the Minotaur.

I knew the senior campers and counselors were watching me, trying to decide who my dad was, but they weren‚Äôt having an easy time of it. I wasn‚Äôt as strong as the Ares kids, or as good at archery as the Apollo kids. I didn‚Äôt have Hephaestus‚Äôs skill with metalwork or—gods forbid—Dionysus‚Äôs way with vine plants. Luke told me I might be a child of Hermes, a kind of jack-of-all-trades, master of none. But I got the feeling he was just trying to make me feel better. He really didn‚Äôt know what to make of me either.

Despite all that, I liked camp. I got used to the morning fog over the beach, the smell of hot strawberry fields in the afternoon, even the weird noises of monsters in the woods at night. I would eat dinner with cabin eleven, scrape part of my meal into the fire, and try to feel some connection to my real dad. Nothing came. Just that warm feeling I‚Äôd always had, like the memory of his smile. I tried not to think too much about my mom, but I kept wondering: if gods and monsters were real, if all this magical stuff was possible, surely there was some way to save her, to bring her back‚Ä¶.

I started to understand Luke‚Äôs bitterness and how he seemed to resent his father, Hermes. So okay, maybe gods had important things to do. But couldn‚Äôt they call once in a while, or thunder, or something? Dionysus could make Diet Coke appear out of thin air. Why couldn‚Äôt my dad, whoever he was, make a phone appear?

 

Thursday afternoon, three days after I‚Äôd arrived at Camp Half-Blood, I had my first sword-fighting lesson. Everybody from cabin eleven gathered in the big circular arena, where Luke would be our instructor.

We started with basic stabbing and slashing, using some straw-stuffed dummies in Greek armor. I guess I did okay. At least, I understood what I was supposed to do and my reflexes were good.

The problem was, I couldn‚Äôt find a blade that felt right in my hands. Either they were too heavy, or too light, or too long. Luke tried his best to fix me up, but he agreed that none of the practice blades seemed to work for me.

We moved on to dueling in pairs. Luke announced he would be my partner, since this was my first time.

‚ÄúGood luck,‚Äù one of the campers told me. ‚ÄúLuke‚Äôs the best swordsman in the last three hundred years.‚Äù

‚ÄúMaybe he‚Äôll go easy on me,‚Äù I said.

The camper snorted.

Luke showed me thrusts and parries and shield blocks the hard way. With every swipe, I got a little more battered and bruised. ‚ÄúKeep your guard up, Percy,‚Äù he‚Äôd say, then whap me in the ribs with the flat of his blade. ‚ÄúNo, not that far up!‚Äù Whap! ‚ÄúLunge!‚Äù Whap! ‚ÄúNow, back!‚Äù Whap!

By the time he called a break, I was soaked in sweat. Everybody swarmed the drinks cooler. Luke poured ice water on his head, which looked like such a good idea, I did the same.

Instantly, I felt better. Strength surged back into my arms. The sword didn‚Äôt feel so awkward.

‚ÄúOkay, everybody circle up!‚Äù Luke ordered. ‚ÄúIf Percy doesn‚Äôt mind, I want to give you a little demo.‚Äù

Great, I thought. Let‚Äôs all watch Percy get pounded.

The Hermes guys gathered around. They were suppressing smiles. I figured they‚Äôd been in my shoes before and couldn‚Äôt wait to see how Luke used me for a punching bag. ‚ÄúThis is difficult,‚Äù he stressed. ‚ÄúI‚Äôve had it used against me. No laughing at Percy, now. Most swordsmen have to work years to master this technique.‚Äù

He demonstrated the move on me in slow motion. Sure enough, the sword clattered out of my hand.

‚ÄúNow in real time,‚Äù he said, after I‚Äôd retrieved my weapon. ‚ÄúWe keep sparring until one of us pulls it off. Ready, Percy?‚Äù

I nodded, and Luke came after me. Somehow, I kept him from getting a shot at the hilt of my sword. My senses opened up. I saw his attacks coming. I countered. I stepped forward and tried a thrust of my own. Luke deflected it easily, but I saw a change in his face. His eyes narrowed, and he started to press me with more force.

The sword grew heavy in my hand. The balance wasn‚Äôt right. I knew it was only a matter of seconds before Luke took me down, so I figured, What the heck?

I tried the disarming maneuver.

My blade hit the base of Luke‚Äôs and I twisted, putting my whole weight into a downward thrust.

Clang.

Luke‚Äôs sword rattled against the stones. The tip of my blade was an inch from his undefended chest.

The other campers were silent.

I lowered my sword. ‚ÄúUm, sorry.‚Äù

For a moment, Luke was too stunned to speak.

‚ÄúSorry?‚Äù His scarred face broke into a grin. ‚ÄúBy the gods, Percy, why are you sorry? Show me that again!‚Äù

I didn‚Äôt want to. The short burst of manic energy had completely abandoned me. But Luke insisted.

This time, there was no contest. The moment our swords connected, Luke hit my hilt and sent my weapon skidding across the floor.

After a long pause, somebody in the audience said, ‚ÄúBeginner‚Äôs luck?‚Äù

Luke wiped the sweat off his brow. He appraised me with an entirely new interest. ‚ÄúMaybe,‚Äù he said. ‚ÄúBut I wonder what Percy could do with a balanced sword‚Ä¶.‚Äù

 

Friday afternoon, I was sitting with Grover at the lake, resting from a near-death experience on the climbing wall. Grover had scampered to the top like a mountain goat, but the lava had almost gotten me. My shirt had smoking holes in it. The hairs had been singed off my forearms.

‚ÄúBlaa-ha-ha! He might as well have transferred me to stable-cleaning duty. The chances of you getting a quest‚Ä¶and even if you did, why would you want me along?‚Äù

‚ÄúOf course I‚Äôd want you along!‚Äù

‚ÄúNumber eight, the silver one, belongs to Artemis,‚Äù he said. ‚ÄúShe vowed to be a maiden forever. So of course, no kids. The cabin is, you know, honorary. If she didn‚Äôt have one, she‚Äôd be mad.‚Äù

‚ÄúYeah, okay. But the other three, the ones at the end. Are those the Big Three?‚Äù

‚ÄúZeus, Poseidon, Hades.‚Äù

‚ÄúRight. You know. After the great battle with the Titans, they took over the world from their dad and drew lots to decide who got what.‚Äù

‚ÄúZeus got the sky,‚Äù I remembered. ‚ÄúPoseidon the sea, Hades the Underworld.‚Äù

‚ÄúUh-huh.‚Äù

‚ÄúBut Hades doesn‚Äôt have a cabin here.‚Äù

‚ÄúNo. He doesn‚Äôt have a throne on Olympus, either. He sort of does his own thing down in the Underworld. If he did have a cabin here‚Ä¶‚Äù Grover shuddered. ‚ÄúWell, it wouldn‚Äôt be pleasant. Let‚Äôs leave it at that.‚Äù

‚ÄúBut Zeus and Poseidon—they both had, like, a bazillion kids in the myths. Why are their cabins empty?‚Äù

Thunder boomed.

I said, ‚ÄúThat‚Äôs the most serious oath you can make.‚Äù

Grover nodded.

‚ÄúAnd the brothers kept their word—no kids?‚Äù

I got the idea he was reassuring himself more than me.

 

That night after dinner, there was a lot more excitement than usual.

At last, it was time for capture the flag.

When the plates were cleared away, the conch horn sounded and we all stood at our tables.

‚ÄúYeah.‚Äù

The teams were announced. Athena had made an alliance with Apollo and Hermes, the two biggest cabins. Apparently, privileges had been traded —shower times, chore schedules, the best slots for activities—in order to win support.

Ares had allied themselves with everybody else: Dionysus, Demeter, Aphrodite, and Hephaestus. From what I‚Äôd seen, Dionysus‚Äôs kids were actually good athletes, but there were only two of them. My shield was the size of an NBA backboard, with a big caduceus in the middle. It weighed about a million pounds. It was a warm, sticky night. The woods were dark, with fireflies popping in and out of view. Annabeth stationed me next to a little creek that gurgled over some rocks, then she and the rest of the team scattered into the trees.

Standing there alone, with my big blue-

There was no way anybody would actually attack me, would they? I mean, Olympus had to have liability issues, right?

Far away, the conch horn blew. I heard whoops and yells in the woods, the clanking of metal, kids fighting. A blue-plumed ally from Apollo raced past me like a deer, leaped through the creek, and disappeared into enemy territory.

Then I heard a sound that sent a chill up my spine, a low canine growl, somewhere close by.

Then the growling stopped. I felt the presence retreating.

Electricity. Her stupid spear was electric. I fell back.

Another Ares guy slammed me in the chest with the butt of his sword and I hit the dirt.

They could‚Äôve kicked me into jelly, but they were too busy laughing.

‚ÄúGive him a haircut,‚Äù Clarisse said. ‚ÄúGrab his hair.‚Äù

‚ÄúA trick!‚Äù she shouted. ‚ÄúIt was a trick.‚Äù

‚ÄúWhat—‚Äù

‚ÄúYou‚Äôre wounded,‚Äù Annabeth told me. ‚ÄúQuick, Percy, get in the water.‚Äù

Instantly, I felt better. I could feel the cuts on my chest closing up. Some of the campers gasped.

‚ÄúLook, I—I don‚Äôt know why,‚Äù I said, trying to apologize. ‚ÄúI‚Äôm sorry‚Ä¶.‚Äù

‚ÄúPercy,‚Äù Annabeth said, pointing. ‚ÄúUm‚Ä¶‚Äù

‚ÄúYour father,‚Äù Annabeth murmured. ‚ÄúThis is really not good.‚Äù

‚ÄúMy father?‚Äù I asked, completely bewildered.

‚ÄúPoseidon,‚Äù said Chiron. ‚ÄúEarthshaker, Stormbringer, Father of Horses. Hail, Perseus Jackson, Son of the Sea God.‚Äù

CHAPTER 9: I AM OFFERED A QUEST

THE NEXT MORNING, CHIRON MOVED me to cabin three. I didn‚Äôt have to share with anybody. I had plenty of room for all my stuff: the Minotaur‚Äôs horn, one set of spare clothes, and a toiletry bag. I got to sit at my own dinner table, pick all my own activities, call ‚Äúlights out‚Äù whenever I felt like it, and not listen to anybody else.

And I was absolutely miserable.

Just when I‚Äôd started to feel accepted, to feel I had a home in cabin eleven and I might be a normal kid—or as normal as you can be when you‚Äôre a half-blood—I‚Äôd been separated out as if I had some rare disease.

Nobody mentioned the hellhound, but I got the feeling they were all talking about it behind my back. The attack had scared everybody. It sent two messages: one, that I was the son of the Sea God; and two, monsters would stop at nothing to kill me. They could even invade a camp that had always been considered safe.

The other campers steered clear of me as much as possible. Cabin eleven was too nervous to have sword class with me after what I‚Äôd done to the Ares folks in the woods, so my lessons with Luke became one-on-one. He pushed me harder than ever, and wasn‚Äôt afraid to bruise me up in the process.

‚ÄúYou‚Äôre going to need all the training you can get,‚Äù he promised, as we were working with swords and flaming torches. ‚ÄúNow let‚Äôs try that viper-beheading strike again. Fifty more repetitions.‚Äù

Annabeth still taught me Greek in the mornings, but she seemed distracted. Every time I said something, she scowled at me, as if I‚Äôd just poked her between the eyes.

After lessons, she would walk away muttering to herself: ‚ÄúQuest‚Ä¶ Poseidon?‚Ä¶Dirty rotten‚Ä¶Got to make a plan‚Ä¶‚Äù

Even Clarisse kept her distance, though her venomous looks made it clear she wanted to kill me for breaking her magic spear. I wished she would just yell or punch me or something. I‚Äôd rather get into fights every day than be ignored.

 

I knew somebody at camp resented me, because one night I came into my cabin and found a mortal newspaper dropped inside the doorway, a copy of the New York Daily News, opened to the Metro page. The article took me almost an hour to read, because the angrier I got, the more the words floated around on the page.


BOY AND MOTHER STILL MISSING

AFTER FREAK CAR ACCIDENT


BY EILEEN SMYTHE


Sally Jackson and son Percy are still missing one week after their

mysterious disappearance. The family‚Äôs badly burned ‚Äô78 Camaro was

discovered last Saturday on a north Long Island road with the roof

ripped off and the front axle broken. The car had flipped and skidded

for several hundred feet before exploding.

Mother and son had gone for a weekend vacation to Montauk, but

left hastily, under mysterious circumstances. Small traces of blood

were found in the car and near the scene of the wreck, but there were

no other signs of the missing Jacksons. Residents in the rural area

reported seeing nothing unusual around the time of the accident.

Ms. Jackson‚Äôs husband, Gabe Ugliano, claims that his stepson,

Percy Jackson, is a troubled child who has been kicked out of

numerous boarding schools and has expressed violent tendencies in the

past.

Police would not say whether son Percy is a suspect in his mother‚Äôs

disappearance, but they have not ruled out foul play. Below are recent

pictures of Sally Jackson and Percy. Police urge anyone with

information to call the following toll-free crime-stoppers hotline.


The phone number was circled in black marker.

I wadded up the paper and threw it away, then flopped down in my bunk bed in the middle of my empty cabin.

‚ÄúLights out,‚Äù I told myself miserably.

 

That night, I had my worst dream yet.

I was running along the beach in a storm. This time, there was a city behind me. Not New York. The sprawl was different: buildings spread farther apart, palm trees and low hills in the distance.

About a hundred yards down the surf, two men were fighting. They looked like TV wrestlers, muscular, with beards and long hair. Both wore flowing Greek tunics, one trimmed in blue, the other in green. They grappled with each other, wrestled, kicked and head-butted, and every time they connected, lightning flashed, the sky grew darker, and the wind rose.

I had to stop them. I didn‚Äôt know why. But the harder I ran, the more the wind blew me back, until I was running in place, my heels digging uselessly in the sand.

Over the roar of the storm, I could hear the blue-robed one yelling at the green-robed one, Give it back! Give it back! Like a kindergartner fighting over a toy.

The waves got bigger, crashing into the beach, spraying me with salt.

I yelled, Stop it! Stop fighting!

The ground shook. Laughter came from somewhere under the earth, and a voice so deep and evil it turned my blood to ice.

Come down, little hero, the voice crooned. Come down!

The sand split beneath me, opening up a crevice straight down to the center of the earth. My feet slipped, and darkness swallowed me.

I woke up, sure I was falling.

I was still in bed in cabin three. My body told me it was morning, but it was dark outside, and thunder rolled across the hills. A storm was brewing. I hadn‚Äôt dreamed that.

I heard a clopping sound at the door, a hoof knocking on the threshold.

‚ÄúCome in?‚Äù

Grover trotted inside, looking worried. ‚ÄúMr. D wants to see you.‚Äù

‚ÄúWhy?‚Äù

‚ÄúHe wants to kill‚Ä¶I mean, I‚Äôd better let him tell you.‚Äù

Nervously, I got dressed and followed, sure that I was in huge trouble.

For days, I‚Äôd been half expecting a summons to the Big House. Now that I was declared a son of Poseidon, one of the Big Three gods who weren‚Äôt supposed to have kids, I figured it was a crime for me just to be alive. The other gods had probably been debating the best way to punish me for existing, and now Mr. D was ready to deliver their verdict.

Over Long Island Sound, the sky looked like ink soup coming to a boil. A hazy curtain of rain was coming in our direction. I asked Grover if we needed an umbrella.

‚ÄúNo,‚Äù he said. ‚ÄúIt never rains here unless we want it to.‚Äù

I pointed at the storm. ‚ÄúWhat the heck is that, then?‚Äù

He glanced uneasily at the sky. ‚ÄúIt‚Äôll pass around us. Bad weather always does.‚Äù

I realized he was right. In the week I‚Äôd been here, it had never even been overcast. The few rain clouds I‚Äôd seen had skirted right around the edges of the valley.

But this storm‚Ä¶this one was huge.

At the volleyball pit, the kids from Apollo‚Äôs cabin were playing a morning game against the satyrs. Dionysus‚Äôs twins were walking around in the strawberry fields, making the plants grow. Everybody was going about their normal business, but they looked tense. They kept their eyes on the storm.

Grover and I walked up to the front porch of the Big House. Dionysus sat at the pinochle table in his tiger-striped Hawaiian shirt with his Diet Coke, just as he had on my first day. Chiron sat across the table in his fake wheelchair. They were playing against invisible opponents—two sets of cards hovering in the air.

‚ÄúWell, well,‚Äù Mr. D said without looking up. ‚ÄúOur little celebrity.‚Äù

I waited.

‚ÄúCome closer,‚Äù Mr. D said. ‚ÄúAnd don‚Äôt expect me to kowtow to you, mortal, just because old Barnacle-Beard is your father.‚Äù

A net of lightning flashed across the clouds. Thunder shook the windows of the house.

‚ÄúBlah, blah, blah,‚Äù Dionysus said.

Chiron feigned interest in his pinochle cards. Grover cowered by the railing, his hooves clopping back and forth.

‚ÄúIf I had my way,‚Äù Dionysus said, ‚ÄúI would cause your molecules to erupt in flames. We‚Äôd sweep up the ashes and be done with a lot of trouble. But Chiron seems to feel this would be against my mission at this cursed camp: to keep you little brats safe from harm.‚Äù

‚ÄúSpontaneous combustion is a form of harm, Mr. D,‚Äù Chiron put in.

‚ÄúNonsense,‚Äù Dionysus said. ‚ÄúBoy wouldn‚Äôt feel a thing. Nevertheless, I‚Äôve agreed to restrain myself. I‚Äôm thinking of turning you into a dolphin instead, sending you back to your father.‚Äù

‚ÄúMr. D—‚Äù Chiron warned.

‚ÄúOh, all right,‚Äù Dionysus relented. ‚ÄúThere‚Äôs one more option. Dionysus picked up a playing card, twisted it, and it became a plastic rectangle. A credit card? No. A security pass.

He snapped his fingers.

The air seemed to fold and bend around him. He became a hologram, then a wind, then he was gone, leaving only the smell of fresh-pressed grapes lingering behind.

The guy on the left threw in two poker chips, then said: You shall be betrayed by one who calls you a friend.

Finally, Eddie, our building super, delivered the worst line of all: And you shall fail to save what matters most, in the end.

CHAPTER 10: I RUIN A PERFECTLY GOOD BUS

IT DIDN‚ÄôT TAKE ME LONG TO PACK. I decided to leave the Minotaur horn in my cabin, which left me only an extra change of clothes and a toothbrush to stuff in a backpack Grover had found for me.

The camp store loaned me one hundred dollars in mortal money and twenty golden drachmas. These coins were as big as Girl Scout cookies and had images of various Greek gods stamped on one side and the Empire State Building on the other. The ancient mortal drachmas had been silver, Chiron told us, but Olympians never used less than pure gold. Chiron said the coins might come in handy for non-mortal transactions—whatever that meant. He gave Annabeth and me each a canteen of nectar and a Ziploc bag full of ambrosia squares, to be used only in emergencies, if we were seriously hurt. It was god food, Chiron reminded us. It would cure us of almost any injury, but it was lethal to mortals. Too much of it would make a half-blood very, very feverish. An overdose would burn us up, literally.

Annabeth was bringing her magic Yankees cap, which she told me had been a twelfth-birthday present from her mom. She carried a book on famous classical architecture, written in Ancient Greek, to read when she got bored, and a long bronze knife, hidden in her shirt sleeve. I was sure the knife would get us busted the first time we went through a metal detector.

Grover wore his fake feet and his pants to pass as human. He wore a green rasta-style cap, because when it rained his curly hair flattened and you could just see the tips of his horns. His bright orange backpack was full of scrap metal and apples to snack on. In his pocket was a set of reed pipes his daddy goat had carved for him, even though he only knew two songs: Mozart‚Äôs Piano Concerto no. 12 and Hilary Duff‚Äôs ‚ÄúSo Yesterday,‚Äù both of which sounded pretty bad on reed pipes.

We waved good-bye to the other campers, took one last look at the strawberry fields, the ocean, and the Big House, then hiked up Half-Blood Hill to the tall pine tree that used to be Thalia, daughter of Zeus.

Chiron was waiting for us in his wheelchair. Next to him stood the surfer dude I‚Äôd seen when I was recovering in the sick room. According to Grover, the guy was the camp‚Äôs head of security. He supposedly had eyes all over his body so he could never be surprised. Today, though, he was wearing a chauffeur‚Äôs uniform, so I could only see extra peepers on his hands, face and neck.

‚ÄúThis is Argus,‚Äù Chiron told me. ‚ÄúHe will drive you into the city, and, er, well, keep an eye on things.‚Äù

I heard footsteps behind us.

Luke came running up the hill, carrying a pair of basketball shoes.

‚ÄúHey!‚Äù he panted. ‚ÄúGlad I caught you.‚Äù

Annabeth blushed, the way she always did when Luke was around.

‚ÄúJust wanted to say good luck,‚Äù Luke told me. ‚ÄúAnd I thought‚Ä¶um, maybe you could use these.‚Äù

He handed me the sneakers, which looked pretty normal. They even smelled kind of normal.

White bird‚Äôs wings sprouted out of the heels, startling me so much, I dropped them. The shoes flapped around on the ground until the wings folded up and disappeared.

I didn‚Äôt know what to say. It was cool enough that Luke had come to say good-bye. I‚Äôd been afraid he might resent me for getting so much attention the last few days. But here he was giving me a magic gift‚Ä¶.It made me blush almost as much as Annabeth.

CHAPTER 11: WE VISIT THE GARDEN GNOME EMPORIUM

IN A WAY, IT‚ÄôS NICE TO KNOW THERE ARE Greek gods out there, because you have somebody to blame when things go wrong. CHAPTER 12: WE GET ADVICE FROM A POODLE

WE WERE PRETTY MISERABLE THAT night. We camped out in the woods, a hundred yards from the main road, in a marshy clearing that local kids had obviously been using for parties. The ground was littered with flattened soda cans and fast-food wrappers.

We‚Äôd taken some food and blankets from Aunty Em‚Äôs, but we didn‚Äôt dare light a fire to dry our damp clothes. The Furies and Medusa had provided enough excitement for one day. We didn‚Äôt want to attract anything else.

Great, I thought. We just blowtorched a national monument.

The monster growled. It seemed in no hurry to finish me off now that I was beaten.

‚ÄúIf you are the son of Poseidon,‚Äù Echidna hissed, ‚Äúyou would not fear water. Jump, Percy Jackson. Show me that water will not harm you. Jump and retrieve your sword. Prove your bloodline.‚Äù

She was right: I was dying. I could feel my breath slowing down. Nobody could save me, not even the gods.

CHAPTER 14: I BECOME A KNOWN FUGITIVE

I‚ÄôD LOVE TO TELL YOU I HAD SOME DEEP revelation on my way down, that I came to terms with my own mortality, laughed in the face of death, et cetera.

The truth? My only thought was: Aaaaggghhh!

The river raced toward me at the speed of a truck. Wind ripped the breath from my lungs. Steeples and skyscrapers and bridges tumbled in and out of my vision.

And then: Flaaa-booom!

A whiteout of bubbles. I sank through the murk, sure that I was about to end up embedded in a hundred feet of mud and lost forever.

But my impact with the water hadn‚Äôt hurt. I was falling slowly now, bubbles trickling up through my fingers. I settled on the river bottom soundlessly. I looked at the garbage floating by and snatched an old cigarette lighter.

No way, I thought.

I flicked the lighter. It sparked. A tiny flame appeared, right there at the bottom of the Mississippi.

I grabbed a soggy hamburger wrapper out of the current and immediately the paper turned dry. I lit it with no problem. As soon as I let it go, the flames sputtered out. The wrapper turned back into a slimy rag. Weird.

But the strangest thought occurred to me only last: I was breathing. I was underwater, and I was breathing normally.

‚ÄúWhat?‚Äù

‚ÄúBut‚Ä¶‚Äù I was sure this woman was my mother, or a vision of her, anyway. ‚ÄúWho—how did you—‚Äù

‚ÄúGifts?‚Äù I asked. ‚ÄúWhat gifts? Wait!‚Äù

We had been in the Lotus Casino for five days.

CHAPTER 19: WE FIND OUT THE TRUTH, SORT OF

IMAGINE THE LARGEST CONCERT CROWD you‚Äôve ever seen, a football field packed with a million fans.

Now imagine a field a million times that big, packed with people, and imagine the electricity has gone out, and there is no noise, no light, no beach ball bouncing around over the crowd. Something tragic has happened backstage. Whispering masses of people are just milling around in the shadows, waiting for a concert that will never start.

If you can picture that, you have a pretty good idea what the Fields of Asphodel looked like. The black grass had been trampled by eons of dead feet. A warm, moist wind blew like the breath of a swamp. Black trees— Grover told me they were poplars—grew in clumps here and there.

The cavern ceiling was so high above us it might‚Äôve been a bank of storm clouds, except for the stalactites, which glowed faint gray and looked wickedly pointed, I tried not to imagine they‚Äôd fall on us at any moment, but dotted around the fields were several that had fallen and impaled themselves in the black grass. I guess the dead didn‚Äôt have to worry about little hazards like being speared by stalactites the size of booster rockets.

Annabeth, Grover, and I tried to blend into the crowd, keeping an eye out for security ghouls. I couldn‚Äôt help looking for familiar faces among the spirits of Asphodel, but the dead are hard to look at. Their faces shimmer. They all look slightly angry or confused. They will come up to you and speak, but their voices sound like chatter, like bats twittering. Once they realize you can‚Äôt understand them, they frown and move away.

The dead aren‚Äôt scary. They‚Äôre just sad.

We crept along, following the line of new arrivals that snaked from the main gates toward a black-tented pavilion with a banner that read:


JUDGMENTS FOR ELYSIUM AND

ETERNAL DAMNATION

Welcome, Newly Deceased!


Out the back of the tent came two much smaller lines.

To the left, spirits flanked by security ghouls were marched down a rocky path toward the Fields of Punishment, which glowed and smoked in the distance, a vast, cracked wasteland with rivers of lava and minefields and miles of barbed wire separating the different torture areas. Even from far away, I could see people being chased by hellhounds, burned at the stake, forced to run naked through cactus patches or listen to opera music. I could just make out a tiny hill, with the ant-size figure of Sisyphus struggling to move his boulder to the top. And I saw worse tortures, too—things I don‚Äôt want to describe.

The line coming from the right side of the judgment pavilion was much better. This one led down toward a small valley surrounded by walls—a gated community, which seemed to be the only happy part of the Underworld. Beyond the security gate were neighborhoods of beautiful houses from every time period in history, Roman villas and medieval castles and Victorian mansions. Silver and gold flowers bloomed on the lawns. The grass rippled in rainbow colors. I could hear laughter and smell barbecue cooking.

Elysium.

In the middle of that valley was a glittering blue lake, with three small islands like a vacation resort in the Bahamas. The Isles of the Blest, for people who had chosen to be reborn three times, and three times achieved Elysium. Immediately I knew that‚Äôs where I wanted to go when I died.

CHAPTER 20: I BATTLE MY JERK RELATIVE

A COAST GUARD BOAT PICKED US UP, but they were too busy to keep us for long, or to wonder how three kids in street clothes had gotten out into the middle of the bay. There was a disaster to mop up. Their radios were jammed with distress calls.

CHAPTER 21: I SETTLE MY TAB

IT‚ÄôS FUNNY HOW HUMANS CAN WRAP THEIR mind around things and fit them into their version of reality. Chiron had told me that long ago. As usual, I didn‚Äôt appreciate his wisdom until much later.

This crazy kidnapper (a.k.a. Ares) was the same man who had abducted me and two other adolescents in New York and brought us across the country on a ten-day odyssey of terror.

The gods were in giant human form, as Hades had been, but I could barely look at them without feeling a tingle, as if my body were starting to burn. His face was proud and handsome and grim, his eyes rainy gray. As I got nearer to him, the air crackled and smelled of ozone.

The god sitting next to him was his brother, without a doubt, but he was dressed very differently. He reminded me of a beachcomber from Key West. He wore leather sandals, khaki Bermuda shorts, and a Tommy Bahama shirt with coconuts and parrots all over it. His skin was deeply tanned, his hands scarred like an old-time fisherman‚Äôs. His hair was dark, like mine. His face had the same brooding look that had always gotten me branded as a rebel. His throne was a deep-sea fisherman‚Äôs chair. It was a simple swiveling kind, with a black leather seat and a built-in holster for a fishing pole. Instead of a pole, the holster held a bronze trident, flickering with green light around the tips.

The gods weren‚Äôt moving or speaking, but there was tension in the air, as if they‚Äôd just finished an argument.

‚ÄúPeace, brother,‚Äù Poseidon finally said. His voice stirred my oldest memories: that warm glow I remembered as a baby, the sensation of this god‚Äôs hand on my forehead. ‚ÄúThe boy defers to his father. This is only right.‚Äù

‚ÄúPerseus,‚Äù Poseidon said. ‚ÄúLook at me.‚Äù

I got the feeling Poseidon really didn‚Äôt know what to think of me. He didn‚Äôt know whether he was happy to have me as a son or not. In a strange way, I was glad that Poseidon was so distant. There was a long silence, broken only by the crackle of the hearth fire.

Zeus opened his palm. The lightning bolt flew into it. As he closed his fist, the metallic points flared with electricity, until he was holding what looked more like the classic thunderbolt, a twenty-foot javelin of arcing, hissing energy that made the hairs on my scalp rise.

‚ÄúLord?‚Äù I asked.

‚ÄúAres didn‚Äôt act alone. Someone else—something else—came up with the idea.‚Äù

A package. A decision.

CHAPTER 22: THE PROPHECY COMES TRUE

WE WERE THE FIRST HEROES TO return alive to Half-Blood Hill since Luke, so of course everybody treated us as if we‚Äôd won some reality-TV contest. According to camp tradition, we wore laurel wreaths to a big feast prepared in our honor, then led a procession down to the bonfire, where we got to burn the burial shrouds our cabins had made for us in our absence.

Annabeth‚Äôs shroud was so beautiful—gray silk with embroidered owls— I told her it seemed a shame not to bury her in it. She punched me and told me to shut up.

Being the son of Poseidon, I didn‚Äôt have any cabinmates, so the Ares cabin had volunteered to make my shroud. They‚Äôd taken an old bedsheet and painted smiley faces with X‚Äôed-out eyes around the border, and the word LOSER painted really big in the middle.

It was fun to burn.

As Apollo‚Äôs cabin led the sing-along and passed out s‚Äômores, I was surrounded by my old Hermes cabinmates, Annabeth‚Äôs friends from Athena, and Grover‚Äôs satyr buddies, who were admiring the brand-new searcher‚Äôs license he‚Äôd received from the Council of Cloven Elders. I moved back into cabin three, but it didn‚Äôt feel so lonely anymore. I had my friends to train with during the day. At night, I lay awake and listened to the sea, knowing my father was out there. As for my mother, she had a chance at a new life. Her letter arrived a week after I got back to camp. She told me Gabe had left mysteriously— disappeared off the face of the planet, in fact. She‚Äôd reported him missing to the police, but she had a funny feeling they would never find him.

On a completely unrelated subject, she‚Äôd sold her first life-size concrete sculpture, entitled The Poker Player, to a collector, through an art gallery in Soho. At the bottom, she wrote a P.S.: Percy, I‚Äôve found a good private school here in the city. I‚Äôve put a deposit down to hold you a spot, in case you want to enroll for seventh grade. You could live at home. But if you want to go year-round at Half-Blood Hill, I‚Äôll understand.

I folded the note carefully and set it on my bedside table. Every night before I went to sleep, I read it again, and I tried to decide how to answer her.

 

On the Fourth of July, the whole camp gathered at the beach for a fireworks display by cabin nine. ‚ÄúI‚Äôm off,‚Äù he said. ‚ÄúI just came to say‚Ä¶well, you know.‚Äù

‚ÄúWe‚Äôll see him again,‚Äù Annabeth said.

I tried to believe it. The fact that no searcher had ever come back in two thousand years‚Ä¶well, I decided not to think about that. Grover would be the first. He had to be.

 

July passed.

I spent my days devising new strategies for capture the flag and making alliances with the other cabins to keep the banner out of Ares‚Äôs hands. I got to the top of the climbing wall for the first time without getting scorched by lava.

From time to time, I‚Äôd walk past the Big House, glance up at the attic windows, and think about the Oracle. I tried to convince myself that its prophecy had come to completion.

You shall go west, and face the god who has turned.

Been there, done that—even though the traitor god had turned out to be Ares rather than Hades.

You shall find what was stolen, and see it safely returned.

Check. One master bolt delivered. One helm of darkness back on Hades‚Äôs oily head.

You shall be betrayed by one who calls you a friend.

This line still bothered me. Ares had pretended to be my friend, then betrayed me. That must be what the Oracle meant‚Ä¶.

And you shall fail to save what matters most, in the end.

I had failed to save my mom, but only because I‚Äôd let her save herself, and I knew that was the right thing.

So why was I still uneasy?

 

The last night of the summer session came all too quickly.

The campers had one last meal together. We burned part of our dinner for the gods. At the bonfire, the senior counselors awarded the end-of-summer beads.

I got my own leather necklace, and when I saw the bead for my first summer, I was glad the firelight covered my blushing. The design was pitch black, with a sea-green trident shimmering in the center.

‚ÄúThe choice was unanimous,‚Äù Luke announced. ‚ÄúThis bead commemorates the first Son of the Sea God at this camp, and the quest he undertook into the darkest part of the Underworld to stop a war!‚Äù

The entire camp got to their feet and cheered. Even Ares‚Äôs cabin felt obliged to stand. Athena‚Äôs cabin steered Annabeth to the front so she could share in the applause.

I‚Äôm not sure I‚Äôd ever felt as happy or sad as I did at that moment. I‚Äôd finally found a family, people who cared about me and thought I‚Äôd done something right. And in the morning, most of them would be leaving for the year.

 

The next morning, I found a form letter on my bedside table.

I knew Dionysus must‚Äôve filled it out, because he stubbornly insisted on getting my name wrong:


Dear Peter Johnson,

If you intend to stay at Camp Half-Blood year-round, you must

inform the Big House by noon today. If you do not announce your

intentions, we will assume you have vacated your cabin or died a

horrible death. Cleaning harpies will begin work at sundown. They will

be authorized to eat any unregistered campers. All personal articles left

behind will be incinerated in the lava pit.

Have a nice day!

Mr. D (Dionysus)

Camp Director, Olympian Council #12


That‚Äôs another thing about ADHD. Deadlines just aren‚Äôt real to me until I‚Äôm staring one in the face. Summer was over, and I still hadn‚Äôt answered my mother, or the camp, about whether I‚Äôd be staying. Now I had only a few hours to decide.

The decision should have been easy. I mean, nine months of hero training or sitting in a classroom—duh.

But there was my mom to consider. For the first time, I had the chance to live with her for a whole year, without Gabe. I had a chance to be at home and knock around the city in my free time. I remembered what Annabeth had said so long ago on our quest: The real world is where the monsters are. That‚Äôs where you learn whether you‚Äôre any good or not.

I thought about the fate of Thalia, daughter of Zeus. I wondered how many monsters would attack me if I left Half-Blood Hill. If I stayed in one place for a whole school year, without Chiron or my friends around to help me, would my mother and I even survive until the next summer? That was assuming the spelling tests and five-paragraph essays didn‚Äôt kill me. I decided I‚Äôd go down to the arena and do some sword practice. Maybe that would clear my head.

The campgrounds were mostly deserted, shimmering in the August heat. All the campers were in their cabins packing up, or running around with brooms and mops, getting ready for final inspection. Argus was helping some of the Aphrodite kids haul their Gucci suitcases and makeup kits over the hill, where the camp‚Äôs shuttle bus would be waiting to take them to the airport.

Don‚Äôt think about leaving yet, I told myself. Just train.

I got to the sword-fighters arena and found that Luke had had the same idea. His gym bag was plopped at the edge of the stage. He was working solo, whaling on battle dummies with a sword I‚Äôd never seen before. It must‚Äôve been a regular steel blade, because he was slashing the dummies‚Äô heads right off, stabbing through their straw-stuffed guts. His orange counselor‚Äôs shirt was dripping with sweat. His expression was so intense, his life might‚Äôve really been in danger. I watched, fascinated, as he disemboweled the whole row of dummies, hacking off limbs and basically reducing them to a pile of straw and armor.

They were only dummies, but I still couldn‚Äôt help being awed by Luke‚Äôs skill. The guy was an incredible fighter. It made me wonder, again, how he possibly could‚Äôve failed at his quest.

Finally, he saw me, and stopped mid-swing. ‚ÄúPercy.‚Äù

‚ÄúUm, sorry,‚Äù I said, embarrassed. ‚ÄúI just—‚Äù

‚ÄúIt‚Äôs okay,‚Äù he said, lowering his sword. ‚ÄúJust doing some last-minute practice.‚Äù

‚ÄúThose dummies won‚Äôt be bothering anybody anymore.‚Äù

Luke shrugged. ‚ÄúWe build new ones every summer.‚Äù

Now that his sword wasn‚Äôt swirling around, I could see something odd about it. The blade was two different types of metal—one edge bronze, the other steel.

Luke noticed me looking at it. ‚ÄúOh, this? New toy. This is Backbiter.‚Äù

‚ÄúBackbiter?‚Äù

Luke turned the blade in the light so it glinted wickedly. ‚ÄúOne side is celestial bronze. The other is tempered steel. Works on mortals and immortals both.‚Äù

I thought about what Chiron had told me when I started my quest—that a hero should never harm mortals unless absolutely necessary.

‚ÄúI didn‚Äôt know they could make weapons like that.‚Äù

‚ÄúThey probably can‚Äôt,‚Äù Luke agreed. ‚ÄúIt‚Äôs one of a kind.‚Äù

He gave me a tiny smile, then slid the sword into its scabbard. ‚ÄúListen, I was going to come looking for you. What do you say we go down to the woods one last time, look for something to fight?‚Äù

I don‚Äôt know why I hesitated. I should‚Äôve felt relieved that Luke was being so friendly. Ever since I‚Äôd gotten back from the quest, he‚Äôd been acting a little distant. I was afraid he might resent me for all the attention I‚Äôd gotten.

‚ÄúYou think it‚Äôs a good idea?‚Äù I asked. ‚ÄúI mean—‚Äù

‚ÄúAw, come on.‚Äù He rummaged in his gym bag and pulled out a six-pack of Cokes. ‚ÄúDrinks are on me.‚Äù

I stared at the Cokes, wondering where the heck he‚Äôd gotten them. There were no regular mortal sodas at the camp store. No way to smuggle them in unless you talked to a satyr, maybe.

Of course, the magic dinner goblets would fill with anything you want, but it just didn‚Äôt taste the same as a real Coke, straight out of the can.

Sugar and caffeine. My willpower crumbled.

‚ÄúSure,‚Äù I decided. ‚ÄúWhy not?‚Äù

We walked down to the woods and kicked around for some kind of monster to fight, but it was too hot. All the monsters with any sense must‚Äôve been taking siestas in their nice cool caves.

We found a shady spot by the creek where I‚Äôd broken Clarisse‚Äôs spear during my first capture the flag game. We sat on a big rock, drank our Cokes, and watched the sunlight in the woods.

After a while Luke said, ‚ÄúYou miss being on a quest?‚Äù

‚ÄúWith monsters attacking me every three feet? Are you kidding?‚Äù

Luke raised an eyebrow.

‚ÄúYeah, I miss it,‚Äù I admitted. ‚ÄúYou?‚Äù

A shadow passed over his face.

I was used to hearing from the girls how good-looking Luke was, but at the moment, he looked weary, and angry, and not at all handsome. His blond hair was gray in the sunlight. The scar on his face looked deeper than usual. I could imagine him as an old man.

‚ÄúI‚Äôve lived at Half-Blood Hill year-round since I was fourteen,‚Äù he told me. ‚ÄúEver since Thalia‚Ä¶well, you know. I trained, and trained, and trained. He crumpled his Coke can and threw it into the creek, which really shocked me. One of the first things you learn at Camp Half-Blood is: Don‚Äôt litter. You‚Äôll hear from the nymphs and the naiads. They‚Äôll get even. You‚Äôll crawl into bed one night and find your sheets filled with centipedes and mud.

‚ÄúThe heck with laurel wreaths,‚Äù Luke said. ‚ÄúI‚Äôm not going to end up like those dusty trophies in the Big House attic.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou make it sound like you‚Äôre leaving.‚Äù

Luke gave me a twisted smile. ‚ÄúOh, I‚Äôm leaving, all right, Percy. I brought you down here to say good-bye.‚Äù

He snapped his fingers. A small fire burned a hole in the ground at my feet. Out crawled something glistening black, about the size of my hand. A scorpion.

I started to go for my pen.

‚ÄúI wouldn‚Äôt,‚Äù Luke cautioned. ‚ÄúPit scorpions can jump up to fifteen feet. Its stinger can pierce right through your clothes. You‚Äôll be dead in sixty seconds.‚Äù

‚ÄúLuke, what—‚Äù

Then it hit me.

You will be betrayed by one who calls you a friend.

‚ÄúYou,‚Äù I said.

He stood calmly and brushed off his jeans.

The scorpion paid him no attention. It kept its beady black eyes on me, clamping its pincers as it crawled onto my shoe.

‚ÄúI saw a lot out there in the world, Percy,‚Äù Luke said. ‚ÄúDidn‚Äôt you feel it —the darkness gathering, the monsters growing stronger? Didn‚Äôt you realize how useless it all is? All the heroics—being pawns of the gods. They should‚Äôve been overthrown thousands of years ago, but they‚Äôve hung on, thanks to us half-bloods.‚Äù

I couldn‚Äôt believe this was happening.

‚ÄúLuke‚Ä¶you‚Äôre talking about our parents,‚Äù I said.

He laughed. ‚ÄúThat‚Äôs supposed to make me love them? Their precious ‚ÄòWestern civilization‚Äô is a disease, Percy. It‚Äôs killing the world. The only way to stop it is to burn it to the ground, start over with something more honest.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou‚Äôre as crazy as Ares.‚Äù

His eyes flared. ‚ÄúAres is a fool. He never realized the true master he was serving. If I had time, Percy, I could explain. But I‚Äôm afraid you won‚Äôt live that long.‚Äù

The scorpion crawled onto my pants leg.

There had to be a way out of this. I needed time to think.

‚ÄúKronos,‚Äù I said. ‚ÄúThat‚Äôs who you serve.‚Äù

The air got colder.

‚ÄúYou should be careful with names,‚Äù Luke warned.

‚ÄúKronos got you to steal the master bolt and the helm. He spoke to you in your dreams.‚Äù

Luke‚Äôs eye twitched. ‚ÄúHe spoke to you, too, Percy. You should‚Äôve listened.‚Äù

‚ÄúHe‚Äôs brainwashing you, Luke.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou‚Äôre wrong. He showed me that my talents are being wasted. You know what my quest was two years ago, Percy? My father, Hermes, wanted me to steal a golden apple from the Garden of the Hesperides and return it to Olympus. After all the training I‚Äôd done, that was the best he could think up.‚Äù

‚ÄúThat‚Äôs not an easy quest,‚Äù I said. ‚ÄúHercules did it.‚Äù

‚ÄúExactly,‚Äù Luke said. ‚ÄúWhere‚Äôs the glory in repeating what others have done? All the gods know how to do is replay their past. My heart wasn‚Äôt in it. The dragon in the garden gave me this‚Äù—he pointed angrily at his scar —‚Äúand when I came back, all I got was pity. I wanted to pull Olympus down stone by stone right then, but I bided my time. I began to dream of Kronos. He convinced me to steal something worthwhile, something no hero had ever had the courage to take. When we went on that winter-solstice field trip, while the other campers were asleep, I snuck into the throne room and took Zeus‚Äôs master bolt right from his chair. Hades‚Äôs helm of darkness, too. The scorpion was sitting on my knee now, staring at me with its glittering eyes. I tried to keep my voice level. ‚ÄúSo why didn‚Äôt you bring the items to Kronos?‚Äù

Luke‚Äôs smile wavered. ‚ÄúI‚Ä¶I got overconfident. Zeus sent out his sons and daughters to find the stolen bolt—Artemis, Apollo, my father, Hermes. But it was Ares who caught me. I could have beaten him, but I wasn‚Äôt careful enough. He disarmed me, took the items of power, threatened to return them to Olympus and burn me alive. He ran his thumb down the flat of the blade, as if he were hypnotized by its beauty. ‚ÄúAfterward, the Lord of the Titans‚Ä¶h-he punished me with nightmares. I swore not to fail again. ‚ÄúYou summoned the hellhound, that night in the forest.‚Äù

‚ÄúWe had to make Chiron think the camp wasn‚Äôt safe for you, so he would start you on your quest. We had to confirm his fears that Hades was after you. And it worked.‚Äù

‚ÄúThe flying shoes were cursed,‚Äù I said. ‚ÄúThey were supposed to drag me and the backpack into Tartarus.‚Äù

‚ÄúAnd they would have, if you‚Äôd been wearing them. But you gave them to the satyr, which wasn‚Äôt part of the plan. Grover messes up everything he touches. He even confused the curse.‚Äù

Luke looked down at the scorpion, which was now sitting on my thigh. ‚ÄúYou should have died in Tartarus, Percy. But don‚Äôt worry, I‚Äôll leave you with my little friend to set things right.‚Äù

‚ÄúThalia gave her life to save you,‚Äù I said, gritting my teeth. ‚ÄúAnd this is how you repay her?‚Äù

‚ÄúDon‚Äôt speak of Thalia!‚Äù he shouted. ‚ÄúThe gods let her die! That‚Äôs one of the many things they will pay for.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou‚Äôre being used, Luke. You and Ares both. Don‚Äôt listen to Kronos.‚Äù

‚ÄúI‚Äôve been used?‚Äù Luke‚Äôs voice turned shrill. ‚ÄúLook at yourself. What has your dad ever done for you? Kronos will rise. You‚Äôve only delayed his plans. He will cast the Olympians into Tartarus and drive humanity back to their caves. All except the strongest—the ones who serve him.‚Äù

‚ÄúCall off the bug,‚Äù I said. ‚ÄúIf you‚Äôre so strong, fight me yourself.‚Äù

Luke smiled. ‚ÄúNice try, Percy. But I‚Äôm not Ares. You can‚Äôt bait me. My lord is waiting, and he‚Äôs got plenty of quests for me to undertake.‚Äù

‚ÄúLuke—‚Äù

‚ÄúGood-bye, Percy. There is a new Golden Age coming. You won‚Äôt be part of it.‚Äù

He slashed his sword in an arc and disappeared in a ripple of darkness.

The scorpion lunged.

I swatted it away with my hand and uncapped my sword. The thing jumped at me and I cut it in half in midair.

I was about to congratulate myself until I looked down at my hand. My palm had a huge red welt, oozing and smoking with yellow guck. The thing had gotten me after all.

My ears pounded. My vision went foggy. The water, I thought. It healed me before.

I stumbled to the creek and submerged my hand, but nothing seemed to happen. The poison was too strong. My vision was getting dark. I could barely stand up.

Sixty seconds, Luke had told me.

I had to get back to camp. If I collapsed out here, my body would be dinner for a monster. Nobody would ever know what had happened.

My legs felt like lead. My forehead was burning. I stumbled toward the camp, and the nymphs stirred from their trees.

‚ÄúHelp,‚Äù I croaked. ‚ÄúPlease‚Ä¶‚Äù

Two of them took my arms, pulling me along. I remember making it to the clearing, a counselor shouting for help, a centaur blowing a conch horn.

Then everything went black.

 

I woke with a drinking straw in my mouth. I was sipping something that tasted like liquid chocolate-chip cookies. Nectar.

I opened my eyes.

I was propped up in bed in the sickroom of the Big House, my right hand bandaged like a club. Argus stood guard in the corner. Annabeth sat next to me, holding my nectar glass and dabbing a washcloth on my forehead.

‚ÄúHere we are again,‚Äù I said.

‚ÄúYou idiot,‚Äù Annabeth said, which is how I knew she was overjoyed to see me conscious. ‚ÄúYou were green and turning gray when we found you. If it weren‚Äôt for Chiron‚Äôs healing‚Ä¶‚Äù

‚ÄúNow, now,‚Äù Chiron‚Äôs voice said. ‚ÄúPercy‚Äôs constitution deserves some of the credit.‚Äù

He was sitting near the foot of my bed in human form, which was why I hadn‚Äôt noticed him yet. His lower half was magically compacted into the wheelchair, his upper half dressed in a coat and tie. He smiled, but his face looked weary and pale, the way it did when he‚Äôd been up all night grading Latin papers.

‚ÄúHow are you feeling?‚Äù he asked.

‚ÄúLike my insides have been frozen, then microwaved.‚Äù

‚ÄúApt, considering that was pit scorpion venom. Now you must tell me, if you can, exactly what happened.‚Äù

Between sips of nectar, I told them the story.

The room was quiet for a long time.

‚ÄúI can‚Äôt believe that Luke‚Ä¶‚Äù Annabeth‚Äôs voice faltered. Her expression turned angry and said. ‚ÄúYes. Yes, I can believe it. May the gods curse him‚Ä¶.He was never the same after his quest.‚Äù

‚ÄúThis must be reported to Olympus,‚Äù Chiron murmured. ‚ÄúI will go at once.‚Äù

‚ÄúLuke is out there right now,‚Äù I said. ‚ÄúI have to go after him.‚Äù

Chiron shook his head. ‚ÄúNo, Percy. The gods—‚Äù

‚ÄúWon‚Äôt even talk about Kronos,‚Äù I snapped. ‚ÄúZeus declared the matter closed!‚Äù

I didn‚Äôt like it, but part of me suspected Chiron was right. One look at my hand, and I knew I wasn‚Äôt going to be sword fighting any time soon. ‚ÄúChiron‚Ä¶your prophecy from the Oracle‚Ä¶it was about Kronos, wasn‚Äôt it? Was I in it? And Annabeth?‚Äù

Chiron glanced nervously at the ceiling. ‚ÄúPercy, it isn‚Äôt my place—‚Äù

‚ÄúYou‚Äôve been ordered not to talk to me about it, haven‚Äôt you?‚Äù

His eyes were sympathetic, but sad. ‚ÄúYou will be a great hero, child. I will do my best to prepare you. But if I‚Äôm right about the path ahead of you‚Ä¶‚Äù

Thunder boomed overhead, rattling the windows.

‚ÄúAll right!‚Äù Chiron shouted. ‚ÄúFine!‚Äù

He sighed in frustration. ‚ÄúThe gods have their reasons, Percy. Knowing too much of your future is never a good thing.‚Äù

‚ÄúWe can‚Äôt just sit back and do nothing,‚Äù I said.

‚ÄúWe will not sit back,‚Äù Chiron promised. ‚ÄúBut you must be careful. Kronos wants you to come unraveled. He wants your life disrupted, your thoughts clouded with fear and anger. Do not give him what he wants. Train patiently. Your time will come.‚Äù

‚ÄúAssuming I live that long.‚Äù

Chiron put his hand on my ankle. ‚ÄúYou‚Äôll have to trust me, Percy. You will live. But first you must decide your path for the coming year. I cannot tell you the right choice‚Ä¶.‚Äù I got the feeling that he had a very definite opinion, and it was taking all his willpower not to advise me. ‚ÄúBut you must decide whether to stay at Camp Half-Blood year-round, or return to the mortal world for seventh grade and be a summer camper. I wanted to protest. I wanted to ask him more questions. But his expression told me there could be no more discussion; he had said as much as he could.

‚ÄúI‚Äôll be back as soon as I can,‚Äù Chiron promised. ‚ÄúArgus will watch over you.‚Äù

He glanced at Annabeth. ‚ÄúOh, and, my dear‚Ä¶whenever you‚Äôre ready, they‚Äôre here.‚Äù

‚ÄúWho‚Äôs here?‚Äù I asked.

Nobody answered.

Chiron rolled himself out of the room. I heard the wheels of his chair clunk carefully down the front steps, two at a time.

Annabeth studied the ice in my drink.

‚ÄúWhat‚Äôs wrong?‚Äù I asked her.

‚ÄúNothing.‚Äù She set the glass on the table. ‚ÄúI‚Ä¶just took your advice about something. You‚Ä¶um‚Ä¶need anything?‚Äù

‚ÄúYeah. Help me up. I want to go outside.‚Äù

‚ÄúPercy, that isn‚Äôt a good idea.‚Äù

I slid my legs out of bed. Annabeth caught me before I could crumple to the floor. A wave of nausea rolled over me.

Annabeth said, ‚ÄúI told you‚Ä¶‚Äù

‚ÄúI‚Äôm fine,‚Äù I insisted. I didn‚Äôt want to lie in bed like an invalid while Luke was out there planning to destroy the Western world.

I managed a step forward. Then another, still leaning heavily on Annabeth. Argus followed us outside, but he kept his distance.

By the time we reached the porch, my face was beaded with sweat. My stomach had twisted into knots. But I had managed to make it all the way to the railing.

It was dusk. The camp looked completely deserted. The cabins were dark and the volleyball pit silent. No canoes cut the surface of the lake. Beyond the woods and the strawberry fields, the Long Island Sound glittered in the last light of the sun.

‚ÄúWhat are you going to do?‚Äù Annabeth asked me.

‚ÄúI don‚Äôt know.‚Äù

I told her I got the feeling Chiron wanted me to stay year-round, to put in more individual training time, but I wasn‚Äôt sure that‚Äôs what I wanted. I admitted I‚Äôd feel bad about leaving her alone, though, with only Clarisse for company‚Ä¶.

Annabeth pursed her lips, then said quietly, ‚ÄúI‚Äôm going home for the year, Percy.‚Äù

I stared at her. ‚ÄúYou mean, to your dad‚Äôs?‚Äù

She pointed toward the crest of Half-Blood Hill. Next to Thalia‚Äôs pine tree, at the very edge of the camp‚Äôs magical boundaries, a family stood silhouetted—two little children, a woman, and a tall man with blond hair. They seemed to be waiting. The man was holding a backpack that looked like the one Annabeth had gotten from Waterland in Denver.

‚ÄúI wrote him a letter when we got back,‚Äù Annabeth said. ‚ÄúJust like you suggested. I told him‚Ä¶I was sorry. I‚Äôd come home for the school year if he still wanted me. He wrote back immediately. We decided‚Ä¶we‚Äôd give it another try.‚Äù

‚ÄúThat took guts.‚Äù

She shrugged. ‚ÄúYou won‚Äôt try anything stupid during the school year, will you? At least‚Ä¶not without sending me an Iris-message?‚Äù

I managed a smile. ‚ÄúI won‚Äôt go looking for trouble. I usually don‚Äôt have to.‚Äù

‚ÄúWhen I get back next summer,‚Äù she said, ‚Äúwe‚Äôll hunt down Luke. We‚Äôll ask for a quest, but if we don‚Äôt get approval, we‚Äôll sneak off and do it anyway. Agreed?‚Äù

‚ÄúSounds like a plan worthy of Athena.‚Äù

She held out her hand. I shook it.

‚ÄúTake care, Seaweed Brain,‚Äù Annabeth told me. ‚ÄúKeep your eyes open.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou too, Wise Girl.‚Äù

I watched her walk up the hill and join her family. She gave her father an awkward hug and looked back at the valley one last time. She touched Thalia‚Äôs pine tree, then allowed herself to be led over the crest and into the mortal world.

For the first time at camp, I felt truly alone. I looked out at Long Island Sound and I remembered my father saying, The sea does not like to be restrained.

I made my decision.

I wondered, if Poseidon were watching, would he approve of my choice?

‚ÄúI‚Äôll be back next summer,‚Äù I promised him. ‚ÄúI‚Äôll survive until then. After all, I am your son.‚Äù I asked Argus to take me down to cabin three, so I could pack my bags for home.

ABOUT THE AUTHOR

RICK RIORDAN, dubbed ‚Äústoryteller of the gods‚Äù by Publishers Weekly, is the author of five New York Times #1 best-selling middle grade series with millions of copies sold throughout the world: Percy Jackson and the Olympians, the Heroes of Olympus, and the Trials of Apollo, based on Greek and Roman mythology; the Kane Chronicles, based on ancient Egyptian mythology; and Magnus Chase and the Gods of Asgard, based on Norse mythology. Rick is also the publisher of an imprint at Disney Hyperion, Rick Riordan Presents, dedicated to finding other authors of highly entertaining fiction based on world mythologies. He lives in Boston, Massachusetts, with his wife and two sons. Follow him on
